  <div class="trb_ar_hl">
      <h1 class="trb_ar_hl_t" itemprop="headline">Tomato pickers persuade big food companies to sign on to human-rights movement</h1>
    </div>
    </header>
 <div class="trb_ar_main">
          <div class="trb_ar_la">

  <a class="trb_ar_sl_it" data-role="sc_information sc_container" data-sc-c="shareline" data-sc-url="/news/florida/fl-tomato-pickers-20151231-story.html" data-sc-ti="Tomato pickers persuade big food companies to sign on to human-rights movement" data-sc-th="http://www.trbimg.com/img-56855009/turbine/fl-tomato-pickers-20151231" data-sc-desc="The story behind your grocery store tomato: a campaign for farmworker dignity" data-sc-sl="fl-tomato-pickers-20151231" data-sc-cont="story" data-sc-nn="Sun Sentinel" data-sc-contid="85446245"><span class="trb_ar_sl_it_item" data-role="sc_share" data-sc-t="facebook" ></span
  ><span class="trb_ar_sl_it_item" data-role="sc_share" data-sc-t="twitter" ></span
  ></a
      >
  <div class="trb_ar_sl_t">The story behind your grocery store tomato: a campaign for farmworker dignity</div
  ></div><div class="trb_ar_dateline"><time itemprop="datePublished" class="trb_ar_dateline_time" data-dt="December 31, 2015, 11:17 AM" datetime="2015-12-31T11:17:00EST"></time>
  <span data-content-dateline="IMMOKALEE" class="trb_ar_dateline_line"></span></div>
    </div>
            <div itemprop="articleBody">
                    
                        <div class="trb_ar_page"  data-role="pagination_page" data-content-page="1">
                          <p>Sweat drenched his long-sleeve shirt and soaked his thick black hair. Lucas Benitez, 17, quick on his feet and newly arrived from Mexico, finished staking a row of tomatoes on a scorched southwest Florida farm and stopped to wait for his co-workers to catch up.</p><p>Benitez recalled how his supervisor drove a pickup closer to confront him. He wanted Benitez to help unload a truck instead of taking a time-wasting break. But even more, the boss wanted to show who was in control, and it was not the teen.</p><div class="trb_gptAd trb_ar_main_ad" data-role="adloader adloader_ad delayload delayload_done" data-adloader-size="300x250" data-state="adloader_unloaded"  data-withinviewport-options="bottomOffset=100&topOffset=1000000"  data-adloader-position="articleCube" data-adloader-adtype="articlebottomcube" data-adloader-label ></div
       >
  <p>Benitez, 120 pounds and skinny, insisted on staying put. Why should he be punished for his speed, when he was accomplishing the same tasks as the rest of the crew? The 200-pound boss threw a punch, but the teen blocked it with one hand, a tomato stake in the other, ready for a fight. He looked the boss in the eye and saw wide-eyed shock.</p><p>&quot;It was the first time anyone had said anything to him,&quot; recalled Benitez, now 40.</p><p>A farmworker-led movement, with Benitez among its leaders, was about to ignite.</p><p>Benitez's small act of defiance planted a symbolic seed among tomato farmworkers in Immokalee, an impoverished farm town filled with empty lots, trailer homes, crowing roosters and scrubby palmetto trees. The migrants who sought work each day staking and picking tomatoes were about to take on the farm owners and crew leaders and the corporations that purchased the tomatoes they picked.</p>
  <aside class="trb_embed" data-content-id="85455867" data-content-size="small" data-content-type="story" data-content-slug="timeline-20151231" data-content-subtype="story" data-role="sc_item  imgsize_ratiosizecontainer " data-state="">          <div class="trb_embed_media">        <span data-content-kicker="Related"></span><a class="trb_embed_media_link"  href="/local/palm-beach/boca-raton/timeline-20151231-story.html" data-content-media-present="true"><figure data-role=" delayload  delayload_done imgsize_item" class="trb_embed_imageContainer_figure"><img  data-baseurl="http://www.trbimg.com/img-5685534b/turbine/timeline-20151231" alt="Timeline: How the farmworker campaign grew" class="trb_embed_imageContainer_img" title="Timeline: How the farmworker campaign grew" data-c-nd="2048x1365" /></figure></a><div class="trb_embed_related" data-role="lightbox_metadata" >            <a class="trb_embed_media_link" href="/local/palm-beach/boca-raton/timeline-20151231-story.html"><span class="trb_embed_related_title">Timeline: How the farmworker campaign grew</span>        <div class="trb_embed_related_caption"><p>A campaign that began as an informal meeting of tomato workers expanded to take on corporate giants. A look at some key moments in the yearslong initiative:</p> <p>1993: First meetings of the Coalition of Immokalee Workers at Our Lady of Guadalupe Catholic Church in Immokalee</p> <p>1998: Six farmworkers lead...</p></div>        <div class="trb_embed_related_credit_and_caption"><p>A campaign that began as an informal meeting of tomato workers expanded to take on corporate giants. A look at some key moments in the yearslong initiative:</p> <p>1993: First meetings of the Coalition of Immokalee Workers at Our Lady of Guadalupe Catholic Church in Immokalee</p> <p>1998: Six farmworkers lead...</p></div></a><a href="/local/palm-beach/boca-raton/timeline-20151231-story.html" class="trb_embed_related_cta">Read the story</a></div>        </div></aside><p>Those tomatoes arrive almost daily in kitchens and restaurants across the country. Though most Americans don't realize it, almost every Florida tomato they eat comes from a field near Immokalee, just two hours from Fort Lauderdale.</p><p>The workers who pick tomatoes have spent more than 20 years on a campaign for dignity and fair pay, a campaign that has gained momentum in recent years as more food retailers, such as Fresh Market, sign on. Farmworker advocates are now broadening the campaign to other items in grocery produce departments, including strawberries and peppers.</p><div class="trb_ar_cont" data-ar-cont="Article continues below"></div>
  		        
                  <aside class="trb_ar_sponsoredmod" data-adloader-networktype="yieldmo" data-v-ymid="ym_1606467462023723332" data-role="delayload delayload_item"  data-withinviewport-options="bottomOffset=100" data-load-method="trb.vendor.yieldmo.init" data-load-type="method" >
                  </aside><p>The workers have succeeded, against incredible odds, in getting some of the biggest corporations in the country to recognize their plight.</p><div class="trb_ar_cont" data-ar-cont="Article continues below"></div>
  
  
      <aside class="trb_embed" data-content-pgcredit="" data-content-id="85455729" data-content-size="small" data-content-type="gallery"
      data-content-slug="sfl-tomato-pickers-photo-gallery" data-content-subtype="photogallery" data-carousel-options="outer&removePagesOnClose" 
      data-role="carousel carousel_swipe lightbox_container imgsize_ratiosizecontainer" data-content-key="f9be4a0ec61f2043e2c082c0a4913fe6">
      <div class="trb_embed_modalBox" data-role="carousel_view">
        <span class="trb_embed_media_close" data-role="lightbox_close"></span>
        <div class="trb_embed_media">
          <span class="trb_embed_media_next" data-role="carousel_transitionadnext carousel_nextpage lightbox_next"></span>
          <span class="trb_embed_media_prev" data-role="carousel_transitionadprev carousel_prevpage lightbox_prev" data-state="carousel_hidden"></span>
          <aside class="trb_embed trb_embed_thumb" data-content-pgcredit="" data-content-id="85455729" data-content-size="small" data-content-type="image" data-content-subtype="photo">          
          <div class="trb_embed_media">
            <figure data-role="lightbox_open delayload  delayload_done imgsize_item" class="trb_embed_imageContainer_figure"><img itemprop="image" data-baseurl="http://www.trbimg.com/img-56854e4e/turbine/sfl-tomato-pickers-photo-gallery" alt="Tomato pickers persuade big food companies to sign on to human-rights movement" class="trb_embed_imageContainer_img" title="Tomato pickers persuade big food companies to sign on to human-rights movement" data-c-nd="2000x1222" /></figure>
  </div>
        </aside><div class="trb_embed_related trb_embed_gallerywhole">
              <div class="trb_embed_related_credit_and_caption"></div>
            </div>
          </div>
      </div>
  </aside><p><strong>The bloodstained shirt</strong></p><p>A bloodied shirt became a symbol that helped awaken the movement.</p><p>The shirt came to represent the story of Edgar, a 16-year-old tomato worker from Guatemala, who asked his boss for a water break and was refused. When Edgar went to get water anyway<strong>, </strong>he suffered a savage beating and broken nose.</p><p>Benitez spread news of the assault, waving the bloodied shirt to rally sleepy Immokalee into a galvanizing protest in 1996. &quot;When you beat one of us, you beat us all,&quot; he told the crowd.</p><p>Farmworkers began brainstorming ways to support one another. &quot;Everyone was in their own world, Mexican, Guatemalan, Haitian,&quot; Benitez said. &quot;We were not a community.&quot;</p><p>A sense of commonality among the neighbors, segregated by an assortment of languages and countries of origin, began to build.</p><p>Benitez and seven other workers, mostly young men like himself, began meeting at the Catholic church where many attended Mass, Our Lady Of Guadalupe, a cream-colored set of buildings inside Immokalee's nine-block downtown.</p><p>They walked door-to-door to poll their neighbors: What changes would you like to see in Immokalee? Are you interested in working toward a solution?</p><p>They invited neighbors to their Wednesday night meetings at the church. They created a low-power radio station, Radio Conciencia, or &quot;Radio of Awareness.&quot; They opened a co-op to offer food and other basics at reasonable prices.</p><p>They called themselves the Coalition of Immokalee Workers, and they would come to be known as one of the most successful farmworker organizing groups in the country.</p><p><strong>Life of a farmworker</strong></p><p>In the 1990s, tomato workers were paid about 30 cents for picking a 32-pound bucket of tomatoes, the same wage they earned in 1975, preventing most from earning more than $10,000 a year for back-breaking, dangerous work.</p><p>Most of the workers are immigrants from Mexico and Central America. Some are undocumented, although the Coalition of Immokalee Workers says the number is unknown. Their uncertain legal status forced many to fear going to the authorities to complain about mistreatment.</p><p>Some contractors took advantage of this fear. The case of brothers Ramiro and Juan Ramos, convicted of involuntary servitude in 2002, showed the threats workers could face.</p><p>The Ramos brothers made millions on real estate, grocery and clothing stores, and three harvesting businesses. They also supplied workers to farmers, transporting Mexican immigrants from Arizona to Florida for a $1,000 fee.</p><p>But according to the FBI, they also &quot;threatened [the immigrants] at gunpoint, promising torture and death if they tried to escape. And they made them live in filthy, substandard, and overcrowded apartments.&quot;</p><p>Pesticide exposure is another danger workers faced.</p><p>Francisca Herrera, was 19 and pregnant when she worked in tomato fields operated by Ag-Mart Produce, famous for its Ugly Ripe and Santa Sweets tomatoes. Her son Carlos Candelario was born in December 2004 with no arms and no legs.</p><p>Herrera testified in a deposition that workers got little protection from pesticides, sprayed from tractors onto the plants they touched each day. Rules required workers to stay off the field and to wear protective garments, but the rules were rarely enforced, she said.</p><p>&quot;Every time they would spray, we would be there picking tomatoes, and we'd feel badly. And then we'd get headaches, earaches. Our eyes would burn,&quot; Herrera said in the deposition. &quot;I would get sore throats. I always felt like it burned me and my stomach as well, and I would get a rash on my skin.&quot;</p><p>In 2008, the family reached a settlement with Ag-Mart Produce, which admitted no wrongdoing but paid to take care of Carlos' medical needs for life.</p><p>Evidence was piling up. Grievances were escalating. But change still seemed impossible. How could uneducated tomato workers get anyone to care about their plight?</p><p><strong>Taking on Taco Bell</strong></p><p>While some farmers offered small pay increases after workers approached them, most said the highly publicized cases of slavery and pesticide dangers were isolated instances, certainly not connected to their farms.</p><p>The workers had an epiphany: Why not skip over the farmers and approach the food giants directly? Why negotiate with landowners when it's really the large corporations that have the power as they buy enormous quantities of tomatoes for their grocery shelves, Whoppers and chalupas?</p><p>At an after-work meeting of the coalition, Benitez said a worker mentioned a newspaper article about Taco Bell's massive purchases of tomatoes at bargain-basement prices. &quot;Somebody is paying the price for those cheap tomatoes,&quot; the worker said. &quot;And that's us.&quot;</p></div>
                      
                        <div class="trb_ar_page"  data-role="pagination_page" data-content-page="2">
                          <p>Taco Bell was an especially irksome symbol of corporate indifference for the workers: The company's television advertisements featured a sombrero-wearing Chihuahua with a Mexican accent.</p><p>The workers wrote Taco Bell and invited its brass to Immokalee to show them their dreadful living and working conditions. Benitez said they received no response.</p><div class="trb_gptAd trb_ar_main_ad" data-role="adloader adloader_ad delayload delayload_done" data-adloader-size="300x250" data-state="adloader_unloaded"  data-withinviewport-options="bottomOffset=100"  data-adloader-position="articleCube" data-adloader-adtype="articlebottomcube" data-adloader-label ></div
       >
  <p>The coalition reported this silence at a meeting and asked if anyone had an alternative idea. &quot;Why don't we organize a boycott?&quot; a worker asked.</p><p>&quot;It sounded crazy,&quot; Benitez recalled. &quot;We didn't have any economic resources to do a boycott.&quot;</p><p>Who could help them? Many young idealists began streaming into Immokalee when they heard about the workers' struggles.</p><p>Laura Germino and her husband Greg Asbed, graduates of <a id="OREDU0000183" title="Brown University" href="/topic/education/colleges-universities/brown-university-OREDU0000183-topic.html">Brown University</a> in Rhode Island, moved to South Florida in the mid-1990s after she volunteered for the Peace Corps in Africa, and he worked for a community development organization in Haiti.</p><p>College students such as Brian Merle Payne, a graduate student in Latin American studies at the <a id="OREDU0000153" title="University of Florida" href="/topic/education/colleges-universities/university-of-florida-OREDU0000153-topic.html">University of Florida</a>, joined a 230-mile trek the coalition organized from Fort Myers to Orlando, home base of the Florida Fruit and Vegetable Association.</p><p>Payne met students from throughout Florida on the march, and together they organized the Student/Farmworker Alliance to support the campaign. He moved to Immokalee and spent 4 1/2 years there.</p><p>&quot;There was an energy in the air that things could change,&quot; said Payne, now 41. &quot;There was a feeling there of the depths of poverty and depression and alcohol and drugs. But the workers were a ray of sunshine, that they could come in and be leaders and change the world around them.&quot;</p><p>Coalition members began working with the student participants to organize a series of Taco Bell protests in 2004. The students got 22 high schools and colleges to &quot;Boot the Bell&quot; and cut contracts with the fast-food giant. Religious leaders also joined the alliance, impressed with its democratic meeting methods, lack of bureaucracy and peaceful approaches.</p><p>&quot;They act responsibly and civilly,&quot; said the Rev. Russell Meyer, executive director of the Florida Council of Churches, based in Tampa. &quot;They sit down with the entity and build a working relationship.&quot;</p><p>Coalition supporters organized boycotts and protests against Taco Bell for four years.</p><p>Finally, they succeeded in getting Yum Brands, which also controls KFC, Long John Silver's and Pizza Hut, to do the unthinkable: The corporation agreed to pay a penny more per pound of tomatoes and not pass that amount on to consumers.</p><p><strong>No time to celebrate</strong></p><p>If boycotts and demonstrations could work against Taco Bell, the workers thought, the techniques could succeed with other fast-food titans, as well as companies that supplied tomatoes to restaurants, universities, museums and company cafeterias.</p><p>The alliance launched campaigns against household-name brands and suppliers, who signed on to the Fair Food Program in rapid succession: <a id="ORCRP017293" title="McDonald's" href="/topic/business/consumer-goods-industries/food-industry/mcdonalds-ORCRP017293-topic.html">McDonald's</a> in 2007; Whole Foods, Burger King and Subway in 2008; food suppliers Compass Group and Bon Appetit Management Co. in 2009; Sodexo and Aramark in 2010; Chipotle and Trader Joe's in 2012.</p><p>The food giants agreed to pay the penny-per-pound to workers through growers who follow a list of rules, including providing tents for shade, accessible drinking water, committees that monitor workplace safety and time for workers to be educated on their rights during work hours.</p><p>&quot;Our eyes were being opened to the issues facing farmworkers,&quot; said Maisie Ganzler, Bon Appetit's vice president of strategy. Company representatives visited Immokalee. &quot;It was moving to meet workers advocating for themselves.&quot;</p><p>New companies continue to enroll: Wal-Mart joined in 2014 and Fresh Market in 2015.</p><p>And then there is the consumer. Many consumers are concerned about pesticides on the foods they eat, forcing grocers to provide organic fruits and vegetables. But do they care about farmworker safety?</p><p>Carolyn North, a mother of two from Delray Beach, said she checks labels in search of Florida and organic produce, but hasn't thought much about farmworker conditions as she shops.</p><p>&quot;I see workers in trucks on their way to the farms,&quot; said North, 50. &quot;But I don't know how to find out about their working conditions. If I found out a farm was mistreating its employees, I wouldn't buy from there.&quot;</p><p><strong>The holdouts</strong></p><p>There still are two major food enterprises targeted by the coalition that have declined to sign the agreement: Wendy's and <a id="ORCRP012673" title="Publix Super Markets Inc." href="/topic/business/consumer-goods-industries/publix-super-markets-inc.-ORCRP012673-topic.html">Publix</a>.</p><p>These companies say that it's not their responsibility to pay employees of farm contractors. They say the farmers should pay the workers directly.</p><p>Wendy's said its suppliers abide by the coalition's Fair Food Code of Conduct, which means they work only with growers who submit to audits and agree to a list of treatment standards for workers.</p><p>&quot;We simply do not believe that it is appropriate for us to compensate individuals who work for another company,&quot; Wendy's spokesman Bob Bertini said. &quot;It's up to their employer, not us.&quot;</p><p>The coalition has put its strongest spotlight on Publix because of its large market share in Florida, about half the South Florida grocery market.</p><p>A documentary, &quot;Food Chains,&quot; with actress and activist <a id="PECLB005309" title="Eva Longoria" href="/topic/entertainment/television/eva-longoria-PECLB005309-topic.html">Eva Longoria</a> as an executive producer and nominated for a 2014 Academy Award, showed a six-day fast by Benitez and fellow Immokalee workers in front of Publix headquarters in Lakeland.</p><p>And earlier in 2015, more than 1,000 participants walked through St. Petersburg during a March and Concert for Fair Food; one colorful banner said, &quot;Publix: How Much Longer?&quot;</p><p>Publix has responded with its own defensive strategy, including a slogan, &quot;Put It In The Price,&quot; saying the company sympathizes with workers but not the payment system devised by the coalition.</p></div>
                      
                        <div class="trb_ar_page"  data-role="pagination_page" data-content-page="3">
                          <p>&quot;The CIW's Fair Food campaign makes it sound like Publix is unwilling to pay a penny more per pound of tomatoes, which is not true,&quot; Publix spokeswoman Nicole Krauss said. &quot;We have publicly and repeatedly offered to pay the extra penny per pound and more.</p><p>&quot;To give workers better wages, we will gladly pay more for tomatoes if our suppliers will put that penny in the price they charge to us. However, it is not appropriate for Publix to pay another company's workers directly. At its core, this is a labor dispute. It is the Department of Labor's role to enforce the laws that protect workers' rights.&quot;</p><div class="trb_gptAd trb_ar_main_ad" data-role="adloader adloader_ad delayload delayload_done" data-adloader-size="300x250" data-state="adloader_unloaded"  data-withinviewport-options="bottomOffset=100"  data-adloader-position="articleCube" data-adloader-adtype="articlebottomcube" data-adloader-label ></div
       >
  <p><strong>Impact</strong></p><p>The penny-a-pound premium can amount to as much as $100 a week in a worker's paycheck, lifting a farmworker's annual salary from about $10,000 to $17,000 a year, advocates say.</p><p>Tomato workers have earned almost $20 million since 2011 through the program, said Laura Safer Espinoza, a retired New York State Supreme Court justice charged with ensuring that the Fair Food agreements are enforced.</p><p>&quot;I thought cleaning up the Florida tomato industry would take a long time,&quot; said Safer Espinoza, executive director of the Sarasota-based Fair Foods Standards Council. &quot;But after three seasons, there have been no cases of forced labor or violence by supervisors against workers. This includes sexual assault, which is well-documented as prevalent outside the Fair Food Program.&quot;</p><p>Coalition founder Germino said the Florida Modern-Day Slavery Museum, a traveling exhibit that includes Edgar's famous bloody shirt and other relics of brutality against farmworkers, now seems as if it is filled with antiques.</p><p>&quot;We're most proud that the museum, for the tomato industry, is actually history,&quot; Germino said. &quot;With other crops not in the Fair Food Program, this is not history.&quot;</p><p>The coalition believes the Fair Food Program, covering 90 percent of Florida's tomato farms, provides a model that can apply to migrants who pick other crops, from cucumbers to watermelons to apples to strawberries, and other regions of the country.</p><p>As part of the coalition's agreement with Wal-Mart, the company agreed to broaden the penny-a-pound increase and human-rights improvements to its suppliers in other states. Farms from Georgia to New Jersey, where Wal-Mart buys tomatoes in the summer, were audited and monitored by the Fair Food Standards Council last summer.</p><p>The council is conducting its first audit of a strawberry farm; its first audit of green pepper farms, where the agreement now applies to 10,000 workers, is taking place this winter. And the program has advised migrant dairy workers in Vermont who are seeking better wages, housing and a day off each week.</p><p>&quot;The dream for us is to create a new agricultural industry in this country,&quot; Benitez said, &quot;with proud farmers and proud buyers who receive the same treatment as an office worker for an honest, hard job.&quot;</p><p><em><a href="mailto:lsolomon@tribpub.com">lsolomon@tribpub.com</a> or 561-243-6536</em></p><div class="trb_eg trb_eg_b" data-role="base_eg delayload base_expandable" data-load-event="base_loadjointeg" data-eg-type="embedRelated"></div></div>
                      </div>
